B000TEPKS4|5.0|During the aftermath of Sgt. Pepper and in the midst of the rush of West Coast Psychedelia along came this startlingly different LP. It was lyrically and musically like nothing that we had ever heard before. It is blues, folk, country, roots and rock, all with a distinctly Southern feel. The band members had played together for many years, thus imparting an air of casualness to the endeavour. Rather than harmonizing the three singers just sing together, over, under and around each other. It could have resulted in a mess. Instead, Big Pink simply became one of the most influential albums of all-time, cited repeatedly by musicians right up to this day. However, even if the critics had disdain (they didn't - they loved it), even if the public ignored it (they did at first), even if it influenced no-one, Music from Big Pink would still be a monumental achievement and one of the records that I have played, and continue to play, most often.<br /><br />I cannot let pass the opportunity to comment on Richard Manuel's fabulous vocals, here and else ware. On Tears of Rage, In a Station, We Can Talk, Lonesome Suzie, Chest Fever and I Shall be Released his plaintiff and soulful voice complement the lyrics perfectly. His premature passing was a loss to us all.

1933633867|2.0|Mr. Graeber's book begins in a wonderfully chatty, discursive manner.  It is engaging; it is like story-telling.  From this sort of story-telling mode, which centers on his encounter with a young and rather naive attorney (she does not know what IMF stands for), and their different opinions about the \\"morality\\" of \\"repaying one's debts,\\" he seems about to embark on a rather deep, important, and rigorous philosophical reconsideration of the very concept of debt.  But this philosophical glimmering vanishes quickly; instead, Mr. Graeber broadens his focus, rapidly and quite impressively pursuing geographically larger and historically more remote topics in world politics, and making grander and more encompassing statements that are more and more difficult to limit to any single frame (economic, moral, political). Indeed, he presents such a torrent of information--a kind of rapid crescendo of historical references--along with rapid-fire judgmental frames about each historical event--that it is hard to predict where he will go next. He clearly has a lot of references of historical facts and figures readily at hand.  He avoids making solid points--instead, his method is to render a series of impressions through the selective presentation of a variety of historical facts.  That is, despite the rather intellectual-sounding introduction of the book, he always avoids making any clear, logical line of argument to which he can be pinned down.  But let's assume that I am interested in improving my understanding of debt, which his book should presumably help me with (according to the title and subtitle). I find that, by around page 6, I need to make a judgment call: Is this book really going to help me to broaden and deepen my understanding of debt in a thoughtful way?  By page 6, I have learned, over and over, that the purpose of debt is to allow capitalist nations to harm native people.  For example, loans to third-world nations in the 20th century went into dictators' pockets,while the poor of those nations are still being punished for failure to repay those debts; the IMF's position on debt resulted in 10,000 preventable deaths from malaria of Madagascar natives, owing to local austerity measures; the French killed nearly half a million natives of Madagascar during a single uprising in 1947 because the locals objected to forced debt payments that repaid the French for building-up the colony; etc.  Debt is \\"victor's justice.\\"  Assuming that Mr. Graeber considers it bad to kill and exploit people, his discussion of debt and capitalism cannot in any way be called \\"neutral.\\" In fact, it is extremely prejudiced and can be summed up by the statement \\"capitalist debt is bad.\\"  Very sophisticated.  By page 6, I get the message: the reason to study history is to find more evidence of the badness of capitalism.  Of course, I have not found anything that I would say truly improves my understanding of debt, or of capitalism, or of history. Moreover, I find that Mr. Graeber's foresworn bias renders his points unintentionally absurd. He may say the IMF's position on debt caused 10,000 malarial deaths, but apparently is unable to acknowledge the fact that capitalist investment (including debt) in antimalarial drug development has prevented tens of millions of deaths.  My experience in reading up to this point was disquieting. I had started the book feeling entertained, and then feeling invited into an interesting intellectual discourse.  I ended up by page six feeling repeatedly bashed over the head by Mr. Graeber's bombastic output, and harangued into submission by his opinions and assertions. There was no room for dissent: his point of view was the only right one; and he turned up the volume so loudly, all on one channel (\\"capitalism and colonialism are bad!\\"), that it drowned out all other discussion.  So reading the first six pages felt like the intellectual equivalent of assault.  I don't care whether his politics are left or right winged. But the fact is that his opinions and politics apparently matter far more to him than does intellectual curiosity. And so this book is not for me.  As an educated reader I have to make decisions based on experience.  By page 6, it was clear to me that I will leave this book unpurchased.

B000002BSG|5.0|If your lucky enough to have followed them from the time of their debut album [[ASIN:B00000295A Emergency on Planet Earth]] (and even I wasn't that fortunate) Jamiroquai were one of those bands you were probably hoping would break into the mainstream. The mid 1990's was certainly a puzzling time for music. The keeping it real ethic of the early part of the decade was evolving into....well a number of new and different musical ideas. But during the 1996-1997 period in which this was released at least there was a melting pot of different musical brews to draw on. A funk revival,gestating during the early part of the decade via hip-hop samples and some rock jam bands was starting to take root more heavily. This was good news for Jamiroquai. Their music always had been commercial..well if it had been the mid 1970's anyway. They key was in the production and craft. They weren't just another rhythm section trying to recreate the JB's or Sly & The Family Stone. They more freely acknowledged the dance-funk era of people such as Slave,Heatwave (to whom I'd make a close comparison actually),Brass Construction and even Quincy Jones' early Michael Jackson productions. The fact they had a singular identity all their own as well was big in their favor. And they have that identity every workout they could give it on this one.<br /><br />  The first song \\"Virtual Insanity\\",one of the few of the more hopeful and analytical message songs of the era is a fairly basic funk tune,save for a light samba style bridge. But do to the changing of eras perhaps it captivated the MTV crowd and,due to this era's obsession with media credibility bought Jamiroquai their own pop pass for that era,getting them international hits and making Jay K (and his hats) something of cultural icons of the day. \\"Cosmic Girl\\",\\"Alright\\" and the title track,with more obvious hip-hop scratching all add to the sophistifunk flavor of the album. The first two were the two other big pop hits. But this isn't a hit parade type album by any means. \\"Use The Force\\",with it's full on afro latin percussive/fender rhodes jamming is of the same type you'd see on their first two albums and \\"High Times\\" adds a slight bit of an edge with a heavy rock guitar/snarling sax solo and a.....well not very pro-drug message when you actually listen to the lyrics. The reggae number \\"Drifting Along\\" is a strong reminder of how that genre is really the main point gluing the 80's and 90's generation directly with the 70's,which was meaningful considering the heavy \\"antieightiesitis\\" hanging on at this point. There are a couple of digeridoo numbers that aren't all that interesting but \\"You Are My Love\\" is another great uptempo and horn fueled sophistifunk song where \\"Everyday\\" and \\"Spend A Lifetime\\" are elegantly crafted soul/funk ballads.<br /><br />  \\"Do You Know Where Your Going To\\",a bonus track not named on the back of the CD is a potent reminder of how close the then burgeoning drum n bass sound was to wah wah fueled blacksploitation styled funk,as both of these musical techniques are employed together here. So in addition to getting Jamiroquai at that moment where they did achieve that success they deserved. It had little to do with their musical style actual,great and underappreciated as it was. It had to do with their pop charts and very two sided press,especially how the press really played up that very iffy Stevie Wonder angle. Honestly,that influence was never as strong as it was made out to be. You'd think this sudden mass popularity would be given to a Jamiroquai album that was really grabbing for the public's attention. That isn't what happened here at all. They just made a record that was a very smooth extension of where they were taking their music with their first two albums. And it was likely just a degree of luck that they were in the right place and time to be successful with it. That fact of it being still one of their most creatively potent albums is why I recommended so highly,not just the fact it was popular. Not a bad place to get into the band. Yet not the be all and end all either. No matter what it is an important reminder when,for a short time anyway Jamiroquai and their sound...came close to ruling the pop music world.s popular. B0059XTUVI|4.0|\\"The Iron Lady\\" is a biographical drama film of the life of Baroness Margaret Thatcher.  The narrative focuses on an aging Thatcher with fading health, reflectively looking back on her life and career.  It was ambitious of the film directors to try and incorporate so many facets of Thatcher's life into a single feature film. This is because several aspects of her life are significant enough to be separate films. For example her rise within the Conservative Party as a female politician or the Falklands War both contain enough depth to be separate films. Notwithstanding this the viewer is presented with numerous major events in Thatcher's life such as: her admittance to Oxford University as the daughter of a green grocer, her progression as a Conservative Member of Parliament, tenure as Leader of the Opposition, and eleven years as Prime Minister of the United Kingdom.  The quality of acting is engaging and this film honourably acknowledges Thatcher's breakthrough political career.  Nicholas R.W. Henning - Australian Author

B0034G4P4O|5.0|The Tintin graphic novels follow an unlikely protagonist: a mile mannered, sharp-witted young reporter.  There are about two dozen of them, artfully drawn, with a recurring cast of colorful characters woven through various tales. The books have been translated in 50 languages.  200 million copies have been sold around the world, but, oddly, not many of these are in America.  That makes Tintin something of a rare gem here, and he's part of my family culture.  I worried that Speilberg would spoil the magic, ruin the brand.  Somehow, he didn't. At all.  The film is brilliant.   Not only did it capture the tone, pace, and geographic sweep of the novels, it also caught so many details -- facial features of minor characters, body posture, personalities, villains, props, gags, cameo appearances, gestures, curses.  It is all there.  The motion capture style made it almost lifelike, but clearly not.  Just like the novels.  It's as if the books had come alive. B00656C6KK|5.0|For many of us who became aware of Doris through her 1950s pop singles it came as a revelation that this multi-talented super-star was also one of the foremost interpreters of the so-called \\"Great American Songbook,\\" with striking performances of the most \\"classic\\" melodies by Berlin, Styne, Van Heusen, Loesser, Ellington, Rodgers&Hart, Gershwin, Cole Porter, and the great Harry Warren.  It's the latter's sublime \\"My One and Only Love\\" that is arguably the highlight of this collection, with equal credit belonging to Andre Previn's rich and warm, intelligently supportive piano accompaniment.  Amazon's editorial comments indicate that 13 of the songs on the present album have a \\"timeless quality,\\" though such a judgment may seem a bit premature.  Even Joe Cocker's familiar \\"You Are So Beautiful\\" (not to be confused with Rodgers & Hart's \\"You Are Too Beautiful\\") sounds like a mundane sentiment and mere melodic fragment compared with a Hart lyric or Rodgers composition).<br /><br />Besides the aforementioned Harry Warren tune, there are at least two other tracks that have proven their classic status, taking their place as \\"standards\\" in the Songbook: the affectionately delivered \\"My Buddy\\" and \\"Life Is Just a Bowl of Cherries\\" (practically revelatory because of Doris' slowed-down reading, which makes all too clear that the sentiment of the title grows out of the experience of one who knows that \\"the strongest oak must fall\\").  The remaining ten songs, most released for the first time, are pleasant enough, with tuneful (if not especially memorable) melodies and heart-tugging or upbeat lyrics that are bound to go over well with a large segment of the listening audience.  Beginning with the third tune, \\"The Way I Dreamed It,\\" the music is frequently weighed down by some of the more formulaic qualities of light-rock, Beach Boys or Carpenters-style ephemera: back-beats on ballads taken slow enough for a \\"danceable\\" 6/8 feel, punched-up synth-sounding bass tones, the dated sound of the Yamaha FM digital piano, the relentless 4/4 triads of acoustic piano and/or acoustic guitar, gratuitous sweetening by anonymous duet partners or choirs.  At times it's a style reminiscent of numerous karoake recordings, and yet Doris so closely identifies with her material that she rises above the mechanical accompaniment, elevating the song to the personal and human.<br /><br />There's never a doubt about the degree of Doris' commitment: she identifies so strongly with her material that beginning with the first track--\\"It's Lovely Up Here\\"--the listener is prepared to follow her to the promised destination.  Music critics who are fans of Doris Day dwell on her innocence, her sweetness, her optimism--all of which seems slightly off the mark.  What distinguishes Doris Day from other singers is, above all, her sincerity.  Because she believes so completely in all that she does, it's difficult--to a degree not found in any other artist--to distinguish the person from the persona or, for that matter, the speaking voice from the singing voice. She speaks with the \\"ringing bells\\" that Frank Capra heard in Jean Arthur's voice; she sings with a quality that is so conversational and intimate that the word \\"performance\\" seems ill-chosen.  Instead of being \\"sung to,\\" you feel as though you've been confided in by a close personal friend--and emerged the better for the experience.<br /><br />Whether or not this album belongs in the \\"jazz\\" category to which Amazon has assigned it is questionable but ultimately irrelevant.  Doris has the expressive gifts and musical command of the best American singers and can practically claim a place alongside Ella, Billie and Peggy--or Louis, Bing and Frank.  Accordingly, \\"My Heart\\" can be heartily recommended.  Just be certain not to stop there.  During one singularly productive period, she recorded 145 songs in a 3-year span.  It's available in a six-CD box set, \\"It's Magic\\" (hard to find these days, as companies have gone to shorter, more profitable single-disc collections, or \\"samplers\\").  If you're a musician (my instrument is piano) there's one inarguably \\"essential\\" Day disc: \\"Duet,\\" featuring Doris with Andre Previn.  Many of us wish she would have continued in the same vein, remaining as active musically throughout the '60s, '70s, and '80s as she had during the late '30s, '40s and '50s.  Still, what she's left us with is an enviably impressive body of work--along with a heart seemingly capable of embracing all who discover her talent and special energy.  (Final recommendation: the movie \\"Calamity Jane\\" along with the song \\"Secret Heart.\\")-disc collections, or \\"samplers\\"). B00003CXE7|5.0|What I have noticed is that the grand scale of epics has diminished mostly in the past ten years. Seldom does a film successfully balance action, emotion, power, scenery into one cohesive story. Gladiator is that one blockbuster hit that is larger than life, yet scales back its grandness to let you experience Maximus' story, his rise and fall, personal tragedies, and triumphs. What Ridley Scott \\"gets\\" is that a film of this magnitude shouldn't just be gore, action, violence and the battles of benevolent and corrupt forces, with one side prevailing. There should also be pathos and emotion, glimpses into character perspective, and larger themes explored.   Gladiator exposes the nature of corruptness in those who hold high ranking positions, as Commodus' (Joaquin Phoenix) lust for power and popularity knows no bounds. There's a sense of mob mentality in the Roman crowd, with their allegiances for or against him changing on a whim. Knowing he can manipulate the masses with bloodshed and entertainment, Commodus is able to play this hand well. At the other spectrum, Gladiator also illustrates the importance of loyalty as a means of living an honorable life, echoed in Maximus' statement: \\"What we do in life, echoes in eternity.\\"  Maximus is sort of microcosm of the past ideals, with successful, powerful but just rulers in charge. Brought down to low status, Maximus attempts to survive in his new found role as Gladiator, while restoring some order in Rome.   There are many things about this production that are top notch: great casting and performances by its stars, a wonderful music score and scenery, epic gladiator battles, just a larger than life epic atmosphere.   If there was one slight annoyance, it was that Commodus was a bit too over the top in his villainy. It seemed like most characters were well-developed, but his just was a little too one dimensional.   Still, Gladiator is an awesome viewing experience, one of those films that draws you into its story. It's one of those films you can come back with repeated viewings. (This review is specifically for the Two-Disc Collector's Edition).

B005UKJX4E|5.0|Disclaimer: let me tell you from the outset that this film is impossible to fully describe in words.  What I'm going to attempt here is to explain my perception of what its underlying themes are.  It might mean something different to another person than it did to me, so if you're the sort of individual who cannot tolerate ambiguity, then I suggest you pass it and this review up.  Having said that...  Sometimes the best way to understand something is to contemplate its exact opposite, which is the meaning behind this review's title.  Fans of the 90s show \\"Seinfeld\\" probably remember that it has been summed up as as a show about nothing.  And that's a perfect summation of it.  There was no character development, no exploration of great themes, no overarching story.  It obeyed a rule set down by its creators, that it would have \\"no huggin', no learnin'.\\"  It really was about nothing.  This film, on the other hand, is about everything.  By that I mean that it touches on the dreams, hopes, fears, passions, emotions and thoughts that underlie everything we do and say.  Why do we cheer a football team?  Because we want them to achieve greatness.  Why do we want them to achieve greatness?  So we can feel pride and exhilaration.  Why will their victory make us feel proud and exhilarated? Because by encouraging and supporting them we share a tiny piece of that victory.  Why do we want to share in their victory?  Because such an achievement is a validation of our worth.  Why do we want to have worth?  Because we want to want to be loved, respected, and cherished, and only things of worth are loved, respected and cherished.    Why do we want to be loved, respected and cherished?  Because those things make us feel like part of a greater whole.  Why do we want to feel like we are part of a greater whole?  Because at some deep, fundamental level we ARE part of something greater than our selves, but our individual journeys through life cloud this truth from our minds.  Thus we spend our lives trying to realize this union that we are all a part of but cannot see.  Trace your own actions, desires, thoughts and goals, both good and bad, back to their roots, and you will find that they all end up at this same point. For example, why do criminals rob banks?  Because they want money.  Why do they want money?  So they can buy nice cars, jewelry and the company of beautiful women.  Why do they want nice cars, jewelry and the company of beautiful women?  So they can feel like they have worth...and we're back to where we were earlier: the desire to be part of a greater whole.    We all want to get to that place.  The question is what path we will take to reach our goal: the way of nature or the way of grace.  That is the choice presented to the characters in this film: which path to take.  In the end they find their ways to the thing they seek above all, union with everything.  This film chronicles that journey, and expands outward to show that their story is also the story of the entire universe.  Old souls pass through death and merge into everything.  New souls are born and begin the same journey.  The process goes on and on, forever.  And, in my opinion, that is what this wonderful film is about.

Conventional wisdom long held that big name, highly athletic hitters and young pitchers with rocket arms were the ticket to success. But Oakland A's General Manager Beane and his staff, buoyed by massive amounts of carefully interpreted statistical data, believed that wins could be had by more affordable methods such as hitters with high on-base percentage, high slugging averages, and pitchers who get lots of ground outs. Attributes in a batter like patience in the plate and the ability of a pitcher to get high ratio of strikeouts to walks, ground balls and few homers are looked at. Given this information and a tight budget, Beane defied tradition and his own scouting department to build winning teams of young affordable players and inexpensive castoff veterans like David Justice. In the June player draft, Beane acquired nearly every prospect he coveted (few of whom were coveted by other teams). The reader comes to understand Billy Bean's trading strategy- acquire players that the market undervalues and sell the ones that the market overvalues. Besides being one of the most interesting insider accounts ever written about baseball, Moneyball is populated with fascinating characters. Sidearm pitcher Chad Bradford is plucked from the White Sox triple-A club to be a key set-up man and catcher Scott Hatteberg is rebuilt as a first baseman. We are told of the exciting record breaking 20-game winning streak of the Oakland A's in 2002, the last game won by a walk off homer by the undervalued patient hitter of Scott Hatteberg. Author Michael Lewis offers good explanations of baseball stats and Beane's economic approach, making Moneyball an appealing reading experience for business people and sports fans alike.

1591396190|5.0|The authors contend that leading companies in the future will succeed not by battling each other, but by creating 'blue oceans' of uncontested market space ripe for growth. This conclusion is based on their study of 150 strategic moves made over 100+ years and involving 30 industries. 'Red oceans' (battles between existing competitors) will continue, but with supply exceeding demand in more and more industries, competing for contracting or stable markets will create cutthroat competition, commodity products, and limited opportunity.<br /><br />A study of the business launches of 108 companies found that 86% were incremental improvements within existing markets, but accounted for only 62% of total new revenues and 395 of total new profits. The remaining 14% were aimed at creating blue oceans and generated 38% of new revenue and 61% of new profits. Blue oceans are created by driving costs down and value up, not eg. simply with production innovation. Example: Cirque du Soleil lowers costs w/o animals, using a single ring, and no stars; its target market is adults, not competing with Ringling Bros.<br /><br />Good blue ocean strategy is not benchmarking and besting across all factors of competition, but focused and differentiated. Eg. SWA eliminated meals, lounges, seating class choices, and hub connectivity aspects, while focusing on friendly service, speed/point-to-point service. The overall result diverged from typical airline offerings, and was not a 'me-too.'<br /><br />The authors also contend that the appropriate unit of analysis is not that of firms - based on the subsequent flops of those covered in \\"In Search of Excellence,\\" and \\"Built to Last\\" - instead, the appropriate unit of analysis should be focused on strategic moves.<br /><br />In developing blue ocean opportunities, consider customer alternatives - eg. driving vs. flying for SWA, or buying a jet for NetJet (offers time-sharing). Focusing on an underserved segment is another suggestion - Curves provides a low cost exercise-reinforcing no men environment with simpler machines, vs. traditional health clubs, or exercising at home. Lexus offered Mercedes/BMW quality at Cadillac prices. Shifting target buyers is a third means to find blue oceans. Eg. Novo Nordisk went beyond the industry's traditional emphasis on doctors' (emphasized purity) and also appealed to patients via ease of insulin administration. Bloomberg shifted its focus from IT managers (looking for low price, easy of operation) to trader/users by emphasizing making their job easier. Still another route - consider complimentary product/service offerings. Eg. Movie theaters offering baby sitting service, NABI buses that are not low purchase price but instead low-cost over the product life (light, easy to repair; lower floors).<br /><br />Idea-generating mechanisms - experience the service or product yourself, talk to disgruntled customers. Examples: The Model T filled the opening for a low-priced vehicle, while G.M. later filled the gap vs. Ford by offering variety. 1400052181|4.0|Henrietta Lacks, a poor black woman born in 1920, was diagnosed with cervical cancer in 1951. At the time, modern cancer research was just beginning to gain steam. When doctors realized that Henrietta's cancerous cells reproduced prodigiously and did not die, they begin sharing (and eventually selling) the cell line. Those cells, called HeLa cells to maintain Henrietta's privacy, are still used in biomedical research today, although Henrietta never gave her permission for the use of her cells for research or sale, and the Lacks family has never gotten a dime. This fascinating book is part medical nonfiction, part memoir - it tells the story of Henrietta, her cells, and her family - and although it meanders in places, it's probably worth it to work through the tangents.

159420229X|5.0|Joshua Foer found his reporting on a memory competition interesting, although the ability of the competitors to remember huge amounts of random data seemed almost alien - contests include memorization of lists of numbers, words, names and faces, packs of cards, and poetry. Next, Foer interviewed some memory champions, and one offered to train him, telling Foer that he could compete in the next year's memory competition if he wanted to. So he decided to try, and that made all the difference.   Although this isn't a self-help book, Foer describes the memory tricks he uses: Depending on what is being memorized, each number, card, letter, or syllable is associated with a noun or verb. Then he creates a \\"memory palace\\" using a place he knows well (his childhood home, his current home, his grandparents' house, his work) and translates anything he has to memorize into images (the more ridiculous and dirty the better, because they're more memorable) and \\"places\\" those ridiculous dirty images in a pre-defined order in the house. Then, when he is attempting to remember some arbitrarily long list of numbers or playing cards, he just walks through his memory palace in his mind, remembers the silly things he put there, and translates them back into his grocery list, arbitrary numbers, or the American Presidents. The title of the book is an example of Foer's use of famous people to memorize cards - at one point in the competition, he placed himself (four of spades) moonwalking (Michael Jackson is his king of hearts) with Einstein (three of diamonds) in his parents' basement.   For me, this is perfect participatory journalism - Foer takes the reader through a history of memory studies and discusses what we know medically about memory, but it's interesting because of the stories Foer is able to provide. Recommended.

Plot and Character are minimal interests here; this is a work celebrating its Setting, the Social and Cultural Environment of the City of Paris.<br /><br />As an artist's purpose is through their craft to stimulate in the viewer, listener or reader an experience sympathetic with their own, often inspired in wonder, our author and director was thoroughly successful with me; I come from this experience now, in love with Paris. The 2009 remasters of the post-Decca/London studio albums (from 1971's Sticky Fingers to date) have come in for some stick in some quarters & rightly so; the one-EQ-setting-suits-all-tracks approach to 'remastering' was a creative mistake which simply did not suit some titles, with Some Girls suffering the most. The original release didn't sound like it was recorded in a tin can but the 2009 version did with the contents compressed to a degrading degree, stripping the original of its dynamic range. What used to sound nice & sleazy was transformed into something shrill & dense. I was hoping that this 'deluxe' issue might feature a decent second attempt at the job but, alas, no: this package contains exactly the same 2009 remastered version with no discernible attempt to improve the faulty sound. This is disappointing, to say the least. Some Girls deserves so much better than this. What is truly frustrating, however, is that the same does not apply to the bonus disc of out-takes. The sound here is excellent from start to finish- I'm not about to complain on that score because the 12 extra tracks are well worth hearing if you're interested in a collection of would-be B-sides in fine quality sound. But is it not ABSURD that the out-takes sound so much better than the album proper? This is not quite how it's supposed to be, is it? If you think I'm being a tad tetchy here, compare this version of Some Girls to the 1990s remaster & you'll hear what I mean. The difference in tonal range is striking. Hell, under normal circumstances I'd be giving Some Girls a five-star rating but this version succeeds only in making it sound like a 3-star album & that just can't be right. In fact, it's a bit of a crime in my book. Are we not entitled to expect it to sound better instead of worse?Has concern for musical quality been finally thrown out of the window? Worth buying for the out-take disc, though, which really does sound pretty good.

B004WCTLNY|2.0|Based on a Phil Dick story, this is a film brought to life by credible performances by Matt Damon (as the ambitious young NY politician David Norris),  the gorgeous Emily Blunt (as Elise, the elusive beauty/dancer whom David meets by happenstance and pursues despite all odds and mishaps), and John Slattery (who can do no wrong in any role as he again doesn't disappoint as the Adjustment Bureau's mid-management enforcer Richardson).<br /><br />The gist of the story is whether we have free will or not. The film starts with a stark \\"NO\\" for an answer. David thinks otherwise, so he needs a lesson and his lesson is delivered in the form of a nightmarish visit from the film-noir cast of the Adjustment Bureau (all wearing fedora hats) and the admonition that David can't say a word of what he has seen to anybody, or else... his brain will be reset, and his mind will be wiped out. If the Bureau is flushed out to the public domain, the gig will be up. (So can the Bureau control everything but the occasional indiscretion of its victims? Isn't that a contradiction in terms?)<br /><br />What's more, the Bureau also forbids David to continue his relationship with Elise since she has a different fate and his rising success as a young politician is not commensurate with her success as a dancer in a modern dance company. With these shifts in focus, it should be noted that people moving from the 2nd to 3rd book may feel a jolt as the 3rd redirects the focus which will still have the romance but not as much.  This could be an issue for those who were more drawn into the romance than the other themes of the book.<br /><br />Overall, Catching Fire is a great book and worth your time to read if you've started the Hunger Games trilogy.  Whether you're a teenager or adult, this is a series worth reading.  I've completed the series and feel a sense of loss that I don't have another  book in the series to read.  The author has created a haunting dystopian world with lots of themes about society and the human spirit.  Catching Fire, while not perfect in my mind, is a continuation of that and continues to paint a vivid world where I feel I've been with Katniss throughout the whole adventure rather than just observing.

B003XF1XOQ|5.0|I recently have had the honor to read The Hunger Games trilogy over the past 3 weeks and I have to say of the 3 books, the first and third are truly the best in the series.  Since there are well over a thousand reviews on this site, I will try to keep my observations simple.  PROS  +  Storyline is fresh, intense and gripping.  The author has done an excellent job making you feel you're alongside Katniss in this final chapter of the trilogy.  There were a few times I had a physical reaction based on some of the things I read in this book and that to me is a pure indicator of how well written it is  +  Lots of turns and twists.  This is a wild ride where you will wonder how it will end up until the last page.  While I predicted a couple of the events, I genuinely was surprised with how some other things occurred.  +  The style of writing is engaging and keeps you involved.  I applaud the author on making some of the more mundane parts of the story feel interesting.  +  Characters are very fleshed out at this point and it is amazing to watch them evolve over the course of the trilogy.    CON  +  Biggest Con is the Epilogue.  I won't ruin things for you I promise.  My issue isn't about WHAT happens which I think is some of the source of the more mixed reviews but how rushed it is.  The last few chapters easily could have and should have been fleshed out with 50 more pages but weren't.  I felt like the author ran out of energy and just finished the book without giving the same amount of care  she gave through 98% of the trilogy.  As a result, you as the reader are expected to just take her word for some things.  I think this was a lost opportunity to making this truly an epic conclusion which as resulted in feeling a slightly abrupt ending.  COUNTERPOINT ON SOME CRITICISMS  I've read some interesting reviews on frustration on character development and I wanted to provide a quick response on my thoughts on some reviews I've read.  I will keep this SPOILER FREE  In terms of the character development, I heard some people feel they fell out of love with some of the main characters in this book or were frustrated how they were reacting.  My issue with that though is the fact that given everything that has happened over the last couple of books, I fully expect to see the changes some of the people made in this book and was glad that it was acknowledged.  To be honest, all of the characters in the trilogy have been through hell and back.  The odds continued to mount and the consequences were significantly higher and built up from the previous 2 books.  You can't get through all that without going unscathed.  I think some people wanted people to be 'superheroes' which in my mind they still were but were disappointed with more of the characters limitations and weaknesses revealed.  I feel that giving these people more humanizing qualities allowed me to relate to them and made them true heroes that they were.  Another observation is that I don't think some people appreciate how much of an internal battle can be as intense, if not moreso, than an external battle.  Whereas the first two have more of a balance of action and internal struggles, I would say the battle within is stronger in this one.  I think this rubbed some people the wrong way with how much additional processing was going on internally but I think it is exactly what a person in these circumstances would be facing.  In the first two books, the characters were doing what they were told (more or less).  In this last one, they have to forge their identity for themselves and make decisions without the same structure they had before.  Add in the fact that you're talking about 17-24 year olds who typically are still figuring things out for themselves and conflicted on so many different ways and I think you have a realistic portrayal of characters thrust into some mentally destructive situations.  The only other thing I will say which shouldn't be a spoiler is that the book veers away(not completely) from the thoughts about romance.  If anything, one of my issues with the second book is that I felt it was too focused on the romance conflict going on.  Given the situation and the context, this book puts things in perspective and makes romance take a back seat.  As a result, I think some people who may be more attached to the series for the romantic storylines will be let down by this book.  Not because of what happens but because it is focusing on other elements.  The Hunger Games is not a trilogy about romance unlike some other series books.  Romance is in there, but there are bigger storylines.  Overall I found this to be an excellent entry of the three and, for me, much better than Catching Fire.

1451627289|4.0|Stephen King fans expecting a horror story might be disappointed in his latest doorstopper of a book, 11/22/63, but anyone up for a good story should enjoy it. The story centers on one big what-if question: what if you could travel back in time and prevent the JFK assassination?  Al, the owner of a diner, discovers a \\"time bubble\\" in the stock room that transports him back to the same day in 1958 each time he uses it. After experimenting with changing a smaller event in the past, he has determined that it is possible to change historical events. He also discovers that no matter how many years he spends in the past, when he comes back to the current time, only seconds have passed.  Al is convinced that stopping the JFK assassination will have enough positive ripple effects to make the effort worthwhile. And effort it will take, as Al discovers that while it's possible to change the past, the past is \\"obdurate\\" and throws all manner of obstacles to thwart any effort to change it. Presumably, the bigger the change, the bigger the obstacles. And preventing the assassination of a president would be a pretty significant change. Unfortunately, Al becomes sick (another effort of the past to resist change?) and determines that he won't likely live to see 1963, so he recruits his friend Jake to fulfill his mission.  King does a masterful job of transporting the reader, along with Jake, to 1958 and really captures a sense of what it was like to live during that time. He also captures the glaring difference in the general level of societal innocence and trust in the late 1950's versus 2011 and the advances we've made across areas like women's rights, civil rights, technology.  I had a few minor quibbles with the book, starting with the repetitive use of the word \\"obdurate\\", as in \\"the past is obdurate\\". Not resistant, not obstinate, not stubborn. Obdurate. After a while, it was clear that he was using it as sort of a catch phrase, but the unusualness of the word make it stick out. Also - and I'm trying to avoid spoilers here - he never really fully explains his theory of time travel and the strands created by Al's and Jake's multiple trips through the bubble in Al's diner.  All in all, 11/22/63 was a very enjoyable read and King's exhaustive research into the time, place, and events of 1963 made me feel like I was there.   ~Read in December 2011

Gameplay is solid with a variety of modes beyond basic rules.<br /><br />First, there are cumulative penalty options that allow the player to thwart a \\"Draw Two\\" or \\"Draw Four\\" with one of their own and pass the draws to the next player. You can challenge \\"Draw Four\\" cards also, which when successful will bounce back on the player who sent it your way. If unsuccessful, you draw six instead of four. Ouch. After two hours of play, I'd estimate the success of challenging around 25%.<br /><br />Another mode is called \\"Jump In,\\" which allows all players to play an exact copy of a card already in play, regardless of whose turn it is. If you are player 1 and player 3 plays a Red 8 and you also have a Red-8 in your hand, you can play this without waiting through player 4's turn. Using \\"Jump In\\" also shifts control of the table to you, so once you place your card, player 2 is up next. The \\"Jump In\\" button disappears fast unfortunately. I missed it a few times. Needs a tweak in future updates.<br /><br />Finally, there's 7-0 mode that will probably drive you crazy. Nothing is worse than calling Uno and being forced to play a 0 that gives your sweet 1-card hand to another player and sticks you with an unlucky player's hand of 15. If you drop a 0-numbered card, all players' hands shift to the next player. If you drop a 7-numbered card you switch hands with the player of your choice. Sometimes you don't have a choice on playing a 7 or 0 and end up in a situation you don't want to be in. Interesting play mode though. I give it that.<br /><br />Tournament play helps you adjust to all the game variations gradually. As you progress, the number of players increases, new modes are introduced, and modes are combined. In the last round, all play modes are in simultaneous effect. It's wild - at least as wild as a game of Uno can get. When you win games, you unlock new backgrounds and character icons. Who doesn't like unlockable content as trophies?<br /><br />Visual design is straight-forward and efficient with enough open space for the variety of background image options. It auto-saved though, so that's nice.<br /><br />AI seems fair. I never got the feeling the odds were unrealistically stacked against me. My only issue with this game is the card interaction. It's not very responsive and often selects the wrong card. 159514188X|5.0|When I read Thirteen Reasons Why, I felt as if I had become Clay Jensen. It was like he had moved into my mind and heart. Clay listened to Hannah's tapes with such intensity, quickly moving from one tape to the next. In a similar fashion, I read the entire book in one long evening, soaking in the words and feeling the emotions. When Clay called out to Hannah, it was as though I were right beside him calling out too. When he openly sobbed with despair, so did I. At that point, I was Clay.  At first, Clay is on a mission to understand why he is one of the reasons for Hannah taking her life. He is desperate to know. But, as he listens to Hannah's voice, he realizes that he should stop worrying so much about himself and his image. Perhaps Clay's own involvement is not the only thing that mattered to Hannah. Clay also learns that both actions and in-actions are involved. Sometimes it is not what you do, but what you do not do that matters. It's a chilling message and one that I took to heart. Don't be shy. Someone may be depending on you to speak up.  There is a beauty in the way Jay Asher presents Hannah's story. The spoken words on her tapes overflow with a mix of emotions - despair, love, helplessness, sadness, hope, confusion, elation, anger, frustration, and fear. The beauty lies in the way Hannah brings these jumbled up feelings to life. Since she has already made a decision to kill herself, Hannah has nothing to lose. She doesn't shy away from expressing her true feelings. Every fiber of her being is exposed and laid out without hesitation. As Hannah prepares for death, she finally lives and stands up for herself. That's right, she LIVES.  The incidents leading up to Hannah's suicide were shocking. I'm not saying that because they were all such awful things, but because many of them were actually small things. In fact, there were a few that would hardly even be on my radar as something to get upset about. They were the sort of thing that may hurt your feelings for the day, but then you brush them aside and forget about them the next day. Sort of a forgive and forget type of thing. But this is where I was wrong. Dead wrong.  For Hannah, these small (and large) incidents added up. They were all so intricately joined together, like a spider web of events, each one somehow linked to the others. Jay Asher's genius comes in when Hannah reveals exactly how all these incidents and characters are related. Hannah has clearly been pondering all of this for some time. She has been living in a nightmare and she knows every single detail, every line and connection. Some of the lines are obvious and bold. Others are fuzzy and faint. But one thing is for sure, they are all very real for Hannah. Getting in her mind as she fleshed it all out was devastating.  Despite the depressing subject of suicide, I found hope in Thirteen Reasons Why. It shifted the way I look at things. It's not just about being nice to people. I already know that. It's more about being aware. This message was shining through on every tape. Sometimes it was just a flicker of light and other times it was a blazing sun. But it was always there. Being aware may have saved Hannah's life. Simply being aware may have prevented all the missed opportunities from slipping away.  And trust me, there were so many opportunities to set things right.  But yet, they Just. Didn't. Happen.  Thirteen Reasons Why will stay with me. It will linger like a stray cat that comes by every so often to pay a visit. It will sneak up on me and gently nudge itself against me to remind me about being aware. Oh, and I will welcome these little reminders that what I say and do really does matter. Ultimately, it ALL matters.

0321811984|5.0|I have been reading Garr Reynold's Presentation Zen blog even before the first book came out. Getting Presentation Zen (The Book) was just the next logical step, since it provides all that useful information you can find on the website in a structured, readable, and easily accessible way. Four years and another two books ([[ASIN:0321668790 Presentation Zen Design]] and [[ASIN:0321704452 The Naked Presenter]]) later, there is now a second edition of the original Presentation Zen book. What can we expect from it?<br /><br />What's new?<br /><br />It's somewhat ironic that the first testimonial in the book states that \\"it's often the slim books that have the most impact\\" when the second edition of Presentation Zen is noticeably thicker than the first. 60 additional pages or 25% more, to be exact.<br /><br />At a first glance, there are still the same 5 sections as in the first edition:<br /><br />1. Introduction<br />2. Preparation<br />3. Design<br />4. Delivery<br />5. The Next Step<br /><br />On closer inspection, the Delivery section has gained an additional chapter, The Need for Engagement, which accounts for about half of the additional pages. The rest are distributed over the other chapters, some of which gained a few more pages that way. Most of those changes are subtle and you often won't notice them unless you compare both editions side by side. References to iPads and Prezi (both of which only came out after the first edition) or photos from TEDxTokyo 2011 are also signs of a discreet update. You'll also find content that Garr published on the Presentation Zen blog during 2011, e.g. from an article on the power of faces.<br /><br />The Need for Engagement, the new chapter, picks up some thoughts from [[ASIN:0321704452 The Naked Presenter]], Garr's third book. In fact, it very much resembles chapter 4, Engage with Passion, Proximity, and Play from that book in that it talks about emotions, mirror neurons, and the need to remove barriers. It's not a straight copy, though, but the same ideas re-explained.<br /><br />Obviously, this second edition of Presentation Zen is not a radical rewrite but a careful update. Things have been made clearer or explained in more detail where necessary, new content has carefully been injected where appropriate. If you know the first edition, it still feels like the same book and you're only left wondering if some specific nugget is really new or if you've overlooked it the last time you read the book. I seem to remember Garr stating that \\"It's the same book, only better\\" or words to that effect. That about sums it up.<br /><br />What's old?<br /><br />All subtle and more obvious changes aside, Presentation Zen is still THE book to read on modern presentation design and delivery. Presentation Zen is an approach, not a method or blueprint that you have to follow slavishly. The idea is to create and deliver the best possible presentation for your specific audience. And while that obviously means more work in preparation, Garr provides the encouragement that it's worth it if you want to really reach your audience. The Presentation Zen book contains all that you need to make this happen, backed up with scientific evidence where needed and supported with real-life examples. On top of that, it's easy to read and Garr's passion for his topic and his roots in Zen come across as honest without resorting to superlatives or over-the-top wording that plague so many nonfiction books by American authors. It's a joy to read and encourages you to give the Presentation Zen approach a try. What more can you expect?<br /><br />The Verdict<br /><br />What has been true for the first edition is even more so for the second one: If you're only going to buy one book about presentations, this should be it. It will provide you with all the necessary guidance for creating great, effective, and memorable presentations. When I'm working on a new presentation, I often open up the book at a random page and start reading.It always helps to either reinforce an aspect I was already working on or reminds me of something I need to think about. In other words, it's a great source of inspiration, even 4 years after its first publication and after having re-read it (in whole or in parts) numerous times.<br /><br />The second edition feels more \\"complete\\" now, by which I mean that the extra pages all add useful information and don't feel bolted on. The only downside, I guess, is that there's less need for buying The Naked Presenter now - but that's Garr's (and his publisher's) problem, not yours ;-) This, I guess, also answers the question what you should do if you already own the first edition. If you already have the first edition and The Naked Presenter, there's probably no need to rush and buy the second edition - unless your copy is beginning to fall apart from heavy use. But if you held back from buying Presentation Zen until now - don't wait any longer. Your audience will be grateful.It always helps to either reinforce an aspect I was already working on or reminds me of something I need to think about. But if you held back from buying Presentation Zen until now - don't wait any longer. Your audience will be grateful.

B004DI7HZ6|4.0|I had this book on my price drop watchlist, and as soon as it went down to $9.99, I snatched it up.  Then I read the reviews again.  Where did all these 1-stars come from.  With a bit of trepidation, I started reading.<br /><br />OK,this book has a leisurely pace.  There are bits of action surrounded by huge swaths of character development, history, and description.  There is romance, but of the passionate, romantic, not overly naughty variety versus the passionate, bodice ripping, down 'n' dirty variety. (Thank you Ms. Harkness!!)  These characters truly love each other and don't waste time with the whole should-I-trust-him, what-if-she-this, what-if-he-that, etc that turned me off of romances in the first place.  Two strong willed characters can be attracted to each other without fighting the entire time.  (Again, Thank you Ms. Harkness!!)  Hey, if this is true love and meant to be, then just get along already!<br /><br />One other thing I truly appreciate about this book is that it's intelligent!  I don't mind mindless action once in a while, but this story weaves together the supernatural (witches, daemons, vampires) with science and DNA and actual reasons why this supernatural stuff can exist in a world alongside physics.  The world building was extremely well thought out.<br /><br />So, if you like your pacing light speed, your action explosive, and your romance explicit, then maybe this story won't work for you.  If you want a well thought out story with fully fleshed out characters and an intriguing (albeit leisurely paced) plot, then you should give this a try.  I only gave 4 stars because the pacing really was a little slow in parts - it worked most of the time for me because there were character development reasons for some of the slower bits, but still...<br /><br />I'm really looking forward to the sequel!

159420229X|5.0|When journalist Joshua Foer first set out to learn about the quirky subculture of memory circuit training, he had no idea that it would become a personal obsession. In addition to this obsession resulting in his winning the U.S. Memory Championship, it also culminated in an unforgettable book.<br /><br />On one level, _Moonwalking With Einstein_ provides a unique journalistic exploration of memory's cultural history, limits, potential, and tricks brilliantly wrapped in an everyday-man-turned-champion adventure story. On another level, it shows how our memories are ultimately what make us human. As Joshua discovers:<br />\\"How we perceive the world and how we act in it are products of how and what we remember. We're all just a bundle of habits shaped by our memories. And to the extent that we control our lives, we do so by gradually altering those habits, which is to say the networks of our memory....Now more than ever, as the role of memory in our culture erodes at a faster pace than ever before, we need to cultivate our ability to remember. Our memories make us who we are.\\" (p. 270)<br /><br />Sure, this book can teach you lots of tricks to help you remember better. (For example, it details the memory-training technique of converting pieces of information into vivid person/action/object images and then storing them in \\"memory palaces\\" for easy retrieval and recall; this technique was behind Joshua's winning U.S. Memory Championship move where he recalled the last three cards in a deck using his indelible image of Bill Clinton copulating with a basketball in the shower.) And, it will also educate you as it entertains you. But, more importantly, it delivers the message that \\"memory training is not just for the sake of performing party tricks; it's about nurturing something profoundly and essentially human.\\" (p.  270)<br /><br />And, speaking of remembering...don't forget the name Joshua Foer. (You can even use the author's memory coach's mnemonic trick of imaging being \\"joshed\\" by the author and then breaking into four pieces in response.) B004J4WKTW|5.0|There have been very few books I've read recently that I've literally had trouble putting down when I know there are more important things to do but this was one of them.  If you have not yet read this book, drop whatever you're doing, and go out and buy a copy.  Right now.  It's that good.  What makes this novel the phenomenon it is, is that it is a pure pleasure read.  It's just fun.  Everything about the `Night Circus' pulls you in and makes you want to explore more.  Which is the exact atmosphere necessary for truly enjoying Morgenstern's debut novel.<br /><br />The actual plot revolves around Celia Bowen and Marco Alisdair who have been pitted against each other by their respective teachers to see which one is stronger.  There is really no better way to describe them than \\"magicians\\" but that's not quit what they are.  While what they do might as well be considered magic it is explained in detail as being simple manipulation of the universe around them, more scientific than magical.  Don't fall into the trap that this is just another book about dueling magicians, because it is much more than this.  It is also not the story of good vs. evil you might expect.  No one side is purely the winner with the opponent being the obvious villain.  In fact, there's really nothing \\"bad\\" to cheer against while you're reading (although you could make the argument the two teachers starting the challenge are the villains of the story).  Instead of your Harry Potter-esque story (or Merlin vs. Morgana if you want to go back to the `original') battle of good magic vs. dark magic, you are given a story about real people with extraordinary talents and how those talents shape not only who they are, but their future and the future of those around them.<br /><br />Everyone who takes part in the night circus is unique, patrons included.  The circus itself is what makes the book what it is.  Morgenstern's descriptions of the circus itself could drive the book forward, no plot necessary.  She creates a fantastic world you can't help but get lost in.  The circus grows throughout the book, new tents and new attractions added.  There is always something new to explore.  The descriptions are vivid enough you feel like you're walking through the rows of tents yourself, which makes it even more disappointing when the book is finished and your time at the night circus is done.<br /><br />The way the book is compiled also adds to the effect.  Morgenstern describes the circus as a series of individual tents connect by pathways, and this almost seems to describe the way the book is written.  She gives us the story from different perspectives that all work together and show how the circus has touched different lives over the course of its life.  Because these perspectives jump through time it is a rather dangerous way to write a book.  Writing from different perspectives and making each one sound as vivid as the next is difficult enough, but then jumping through time as well can lead to all sorts of problems in keeping the plot accurate and avoiding holes or inconsistencies.  Morgenstern does it expertly though.  Each section flows seamlessly into the next and there is not a plot hole to be found.<br /><br />This book was a fantastic read, the kind I hope will very quickly become a cult classic.  It was pure fun.  I look forward to more of Morgenstern's work in the future, and hope (maybe a little selfishly) she'll somehow manage to take us back to the night circus in a future book.tic world you can't help but get lost in. B005UT29Z0|4.0|I really liked MARGIN CALL (dir. J. C. Chandor), but I have to admit I don't really understand it.  The plot revolves around a young risk analyst (Zachary Quinto) who discovers that his investment banking firm has misinterpreted its own equations and is on the verge of total collapse.  What ensues is a night of tension-filled meetings staffed by company higher-ups (Kevin Spacey, Paul Bettany, Demi Moore) and eventually the CEO (Jeremy Irons).  As Irons explains it, they have three choices:  1) be first, 2) be smarter, or 3) cheat.  He rules out cheating, says he can't count on being smarter than anyone else, which leaves him with being first.  So the plan is to dump billions of dollars of unsecured junk on an unsuspecting market, which will save the firm even as it plunges all of Wall Street into chaos.<br /><br />As someone with no expertise in either economics or investment banking, I found myself struggling to understand exactly what was going down at MBS, the fictionalized Lehman-style investment house portrayed in the film.  Several characters try several times to explain it, but not very effectively.  At one point Jeremy Irons asks Spacey and Quinto to \\"explain it as if you're speaking to a child,\\" which gave me hope.  But phrases like \\"historic volatility levels\\" get in the way of making any of this understandable.  I get the overall picture - MBS (like Lehman) was making paper trades backed by worthless mortgages (among other low-rated vehicles) which they were playing off against each other like the proverbial house of cards.  When Quinto discovers the fatal flaw in their statistics, we know it will all come crashing down.  The question is whether MBS will put its own survival above that of its customers (and the American people).<br /><br />MARGIN CALL is a thrilling film, although it admittedly takes a bit of concentration to keep up with what's going on.  The performances (Irons, Spacey, and Quinto especially) are exceptional, and I have no doubt the film accurately reflects the mood of firms like Lehman that played a major role in the collapse of our economy in 2008.  But this is not a subject matter that easily translates to story - as such, I never found myself really connecting with any of these characters.  One moment - when Spacey embraces his dying dog at the vet's office - stands out as a glimpse of humanity in what is a very cold and merciless world.  I liked that.  It helped me relate to Spacey's character.  However, ultimately Spacey joins the others in a willingness to sell out humanity for cold, hard cash.  The world of MBS (like Wall Street in the \\"real world\\") is totally fixated on money - making it, holding onto it, and turning it into more money.<br /><br />There's one nice moment when two characters look out at the people on the streets of Manhattan and wish they could be as innocent of the horror that's about to be unleashed by MBS (and other Wall Street firms).  But Paul Bettany says it best:  \\"People wanna live like this in their cars and big [******] houses they can't even pay for. The only reason that they all get to continue living like kings is `cause we got our fingers on the scales in their favor. I take my hand off and then the whole world gets really [*******] fair really [*******] quickly and nobody actually wants that.\\"  This is the heart of MARGIN CALL.  We blame the 2008 crash on greedy Wall Street bankers and financiers, but they're only a reflection of the rest of us who want what we can't afford.<br /><br />MARGIN CALL is a good film, even if I don't understand it as well as I'd like.  It's also a film that will make you think about the world we live in.  I do recommend it.s the mood of firms like Lehman that played a major role in the collapse of our economy in 2008.  But this is not a subject matter that easily translates to story - as such, I never found myself really connecting with any of these characters.  One moment - when Spacey embraces his dying dog at the vet's office - stands out as a glimpse of humanity in what is a very cold and merciless world.  I liked that.  It helped me relate to Spacey's character.  However, ultimately Spacey joins the others in a willingness to sell out humanity for cold, hard cash.  The world of MBS (like Wall Street in the \\"real world\\") is totally fixated on money - making it, holding onto it, and turning it into more money.    There's one nice moment when two characters look out at the people on the streets of Manhattan and wish they could be as innocent of the horror that's about to be unleashed by MBS (and other Wall Street firms).  But Paul Bettany says it best:  \\"People wanna live like this in their cars and big [******] houses they can't even pay for. The only reason that they all get to continue living like kings is `cause we got our fingers on the scales in their favor. I take my hand off and then the whole world gets really [*******] fair really [*******] quickly and nobody actually wants that.\\"  This is the heart of MARGIN CALL.  We blame the 2008 crash on greedy Wall Street bankers and financiers, but they're only a reflection of the rest of us who want what we can't afford.    MARGIN CALL is a good film, even if I don't understand it as well as I'd like.  It's also a film that will make you think about the world we live in.  I do recommend it.

0007263686|4.0|This is an elegantly written, consistently gripping account of Churchill's tenure as wartime prime minister.<br /><br />The author's central purpose in the book is to demonstrate Churchill's historical greatness. However, it is the clarity of this purpose that is the book's central flaw. Mr Hastings' effort to show the giant that was Churchill is such a dominant theme in the book that it tends to submerge some rather uncomfortable facts. For example: Churchill's deeply ingrained racism and the degree of his culpability for the wartime Indian famine are only lightly touched upon; Hastings deals very superficially, and wholly in Churchill's favour, with the persistent historical controversy regarding Churchill's sacking of Auchinleck in North Africa in the immediate aftermath of the general's successful reorganisation of the British Army into battle groups and his defeat of Rommel; he barely mentions Bill Slim and the fact that Britain's greatest wartime general was relegated to the theatre that Churchill thought least of; and he excuses the prime minister's flights of fancy and disasterous choices later in the war as the result, in large measure, of old age and stress.<br /><br />This is not to deny the importance of Churchill as war leader: his insistence on continuing to fight after the fall of France was a demonstration of enormous moral courage; his understanding of the importance of drawing the USA into the war to have any hope of victory was more clear sighted than most of his contemporaries; his decision to sink the French fleet at Mers-el-Kebir displayed the sort of ruthlessness that was essential to winning the war; his unsucccessful efforts at establishing a democratic Poland at the end of the war show a sense of decency and honour that contrast favourably with Stalin's monstrousness and Roosevelt's disinterest on this issue.<br /><br />Mr Hastings book would have been even more interesting if he had allowed his portrait of Churchill to emerge from this sort of evidence rather than seeking to impose such an overwhelmingly heroic impression on much more complex material.<br /><br />Nevertheless, it should be said that few readers could ever feel shortchanged by Hastings' historical writing, which is as ever humane and lucid, and the breadth and depth of his research is, as always, awesome.eeking to impose such an overwhelmingly heroic impression on much more complex material.   Nevertheless, it should be said that few readers could ever feel shortchanged by Hastings' historical writing, which is as ever humane and lucid, and the breadth and depth of his research is, as always, awesome.

0307959856|2.0|I do not fault PD James for not capturing Austen's voice more precisely. Who could? Nor do I fault her for wanting to work in Austen's world exploring a future for these fascinating characters. I have always refused to read any P&P sequels and yet happily gave in to reading this one because I am such a fan of PD James' writing. I figured who better than James to attempt this?  But... Death Comes to Pemberley is tedious, pedantic, and sorely lacking Jane Austen's light, humorous touch.  First, although I imagine James wanted to make her book accessible to anyone regardless of whether or not they were already familiar with Pride and Prejudice, huge chunks of the book are given over to the re-telling of the original plot. Huge chunks. Austen's P&P is plot-heavy with lots of little prejudices, opinions and nuances and so it takes up quite a bit of space to fully bring an uninitiated reader up to speed, boring the rest of us in the process.  Next, James spends much of the book piecing out historical lessons on exactly how the English courts worked in 1797 and what jails looked like and the hierarchy of the law and Magistrates...and I'm weary just from typing all this. Austen didn't explain her world, her audience already understood it, and so she just got on with the story. I wish Ms. James had.  But what an uninteresting, dull story it turns out to be. Only the promise of a ball, never achieved. No parties, dances, scandalous flirtations, no FUN. Little wit, no humor, just a plodding and somewhat unbelievable murder mystery along with a few modern day psychological explanations as to why certain characters behave as they do. For instance, we discover that Wickham isn't simply a delicious and charming cad... No! He just didn't get enough love as a little boy. And Alveston, why might he be the better match for Georgianna? Because he can quote feminist writer Mary Wollstonecroft, of course. Again and again Darcy apologizes for being brought up a rich, proud landowner, fornot expressing his feelings more freely, heck, for things that aren't even his fault!  Why do I give it even 2 stars? Because of the promise of a good tale that marked the first several chapters of the book. Reading the first section of the book I was expectant, hopeful, and yes, entertained. But it did not last.

0439023513|5.0|I was reluctant to read this book based on all the negative reviews and ended up being surprisingly happy with the ending of the trilogy. This was my 2nd favorite book in the series (with the first being my favorite). Here is the good and bad based on my views.  Bad - If you are looking for a love story with a happy ending that leaves you feeling good about the world than you will surely be disappointed in this book. The bottom line here is that this is a book about war and how war brings out the worst that humanity has to offer. In war, people die....good people and bad people. People also lie cheat and steal to get to their desired end result. Trauma changes people in a way that moves them away from their goals and pushes them in different directions. This is not a pretty story where everyone rides off into the sunset so if that is what you are looking for stop after the first book.  Good - This was a wonderful ending to the series that tied everything together in a way that made sense. Although fiction, the story and ending were very plausible and not sugar coated. After reading the poor reviews I was sure I was going to hate this book and find myself disappointed but to the contrary I found myself very pleased with the ending. There were also numerous comments about lack of character development and I found that the characters were very realistic and changed in a way that would be reflective of the trauma they experienced. I felt very satisfied with the way the story ended and it didn't leave me guessing about \\"what if\\". There was a sense of finality and closure to the story.   I read the first book in 3 days and couldn't put it down. The second book took me a bit longer to get through and this last book started off slow for me but once I got past the midpoint I couldn't put it down and finished the second half of the book in days. I can't wait for the movie to come out, but am sure they will play up the love story side a bit more than the book.   If you enjoyedthe first 2 books and are open minded about the ending, you won't be disappointed. I am thankful I didn't let the negative reviews dissuade me from reading this book. HOwever, I am glad I read them because they prepared me to be open minded and allow the story to unfold naturally instead of in the way my mind would have contrived it based on idealism.

1934356476|3.0|Reading this book is like being trapped in an all-day team building exercise.  There's a lot of good information, but you'll have to skim through a lot of bad dialog to find it. For reasons I can't understand, the book superglues you to an annoying and badly-written \\"programming buddy\\" named Bill, and way too much of what the book has to teach is buried in his dialog. A third of the book could be pruned by nixing Bill, and the book would be more understandable and less painful to read as a result.   Entire chapters are spent on topics that could be dispensed with in a few paragraphs and the beginning of the book lingers on information that an intermediate programmer would already know. But the strange dialog style buries information so it's difficult to skim or skip. This improves towards the end.  The following quote is an example of what you're in for:  \\"Forget about method_missing() for now -- we'll get to that this afternoon. To introduce Dynamic Methods, allow me to tell you a story from my youth,\\" he says. \\"When I was a young developer learning C++\\", Bill muses, \\"my mentors told me that when you call a method, you're actually sending a message to an object. It took me a while to get used to that concept. Of course, if I'd been using Ruby back then, that notion of sending messages would have come more naturally to me.\\" Bill launches into a mini-presentation.  A few sentences later comes this: \\"'Wait a minute,' you interject. 'Why on Earth would I use send() instead of the plain old dot notation?'\\"  Why on Earth, indeed.  It's condescending, wrong-headed, distracting and agonizing. The concept is very badly suited to the subject matter and that's a shame; the author obviously knows the material and the book could have been excellent. But for some reason, someone decided the book would use a tone usually reserved for explaining rules to slow children. I suspect the book's trying to mimic Why's Poignant Guide to Ruby, which is a horrible trend I see creeping intoother Ruby books.  Criticism aside, this is the best book I've found on the topic to date. Bring a highlighter with you and try to ignore Bill. This is the best you'll find for the moment.

B0059XTUMC|4.0|You have to hand it to writer and director Michel Hazanavicius for coming up with this idea and especially that it works so well.  The technical aspects are well done.  Everyone knows the film has no sound, well almost.  There is a dream sequence where George Valentin (Jean Dujardin) hears sounds but he has no voice.  And of course the film is accompanied by an orchestrated soundtrack.  It even has the 4:3 (or 1.33:1 if you prefer) aspect ratio, the same as the original televisions.  Then there are the title cards which tell us what the actors are saying.  Like the original silent films, these aren't used often as the actor's job is to let us know what's going on with their performance.  Unlike many of the original silent films, you can actually see what the actor's are saying because the picture is clear enough to read their lips.  So the title cards pretty much enforce what we've already heard...ahem, seen.<br /><br />The story really isn't new.  It is a mash up of \\"Singing in the Rain,\\" the 1952 Gene Kelly masterpiece and the numerous iterations of \\"A Star is Born.\\"  Valentin is the most successful actor of his time.  Handsome, rugged, witty and very charming.  He meets Peppy Miller, a perfectly named wannabe actress/dancer played by long-stemmed Argentine beauty Berenice Bejo.  As the silent film era transitions to \\"talkies\\", Valentin doesn't.  At the same time, the adorable Peppy rises to the top.  Valentin loses his fortune after his self financed final film tanks and the stock market crashes.  It's 1929 and George is penniless, yet still proud and defiant.  After a struggle with the bottle he attempts suicide but is eventually saved by his very cute dog and then by Peppy.<br /><br />While their relationship remains chaste throughout, there is a smoldering fire within each of them.  Initially, George's marriage gets in the way, but his wife (Penelope Ann Miller) leaves him before all his money is lost.  I watch lot of old black and white movies and even silent ones, so I was not in shock seeing this film.  What may be surprising is that once you get past that, there is a wonderful romantic drama delivered by two talented actors.not in shock seeing this film.
